## Ad / creative examples shown (ad-facts)

### Ad #1 — Nano Banana
- **Brand**: Nano Banana
- **Submitted by**: n/a
- **Format**: image
- **Duration shown in this video**: 2 seconds
- **Hook (first 3 sec)**: A bright orange product is shown against a simple, light-colored background.
- **Product / pitch**: A zesty, smart product.
- **Key on-screen text**: Smart Inside, Zesty Outside
- **Key spoken lines**: None used
- **Visual style**: high-fi
- **CTA / offer (if shown)**: None used
- **Narrative arc**: None observable
- **Why shown in this video**: To demonstrate a "Quick Win" method of cloning a competitor's ad using AI. This ad serves as the style reference.
- **Speaker's take**: "I'm going to be talking about Claude a lot in this presentation. You can do this in other LLMs, but my recommendation would be to do everything in Claude. At least right now, it's like the frontier model. So what you do is you find a competitor ad and you upload it into Claude."
- **Timestamp**: 00:22:28

### Ad #8 — HexClad (Generic Prompt)

- **Format**: image

- **CTA / offer (if shown)**: SHOP NOW
- **Narrative arc**: None observable
- **Why shown in this video**: To illustrate why a generic, short prompt results in a poor, off-brand ad.
- **Speaker's take**: "This is a generic 38-word prompt... No brand spec card, no format template, no scoring agents, no safe zones, just vibes... Why your outputs look like sh*t."
- **Timestamp**: 00:49:12

- **Narrative arc**: None observable
- **Why shown in this video**: To show the improved result of using a properly structured, detailed prompt with references.
- **Speaker's take**: "This is what a properly structured prompt and references looks like. 1,000+ word prompt, fully structured... Brand spec card uploaded, format template uploaded, 7 agents scored to 90+, safe zones enforced, same AI, same copy, different output."
- **Timestamp**: 00:51:09

### Ad #11 — Jones Road (Lip Tint Animation Iteration)
- **Brand**: Jones Road
- **Submitted by**: n/a

- **Key spoken lines**: None used
- **Visual style**: high-fi
- **CTA / offer (if shown)**: None used
- **Narrative arc**: None observable
- **Why shown in this video**: To illustrate the iterative process of refining AI-generated animations by providing feedback to the model.
- **Speaker's take**: "You likely won't one-shot this... You're going to upload the animation output plus my original start frame and end frame for reference. Describe what's wrong with the animation... Give me a corrected Veo 3.1 JSON prompt that fixes these issues. Keep everything that worked well."
- **Timestamp**: 01:08:18

### Ad #14 — Jones Road (Shower Gel Multiplied)
- **Brand**: Jones Road
- **Submitted by**: n/a
- **Format**: image
- **Duration shown in this video**: 2 seconds

- **Product / pitch**: A shower gel for sensitive skin.
- **Key on-screen text**: (Headline Ad) It's Not Your Sensitive Skin, It's Your Shower Gel. (Story Ad) Your Shower Stable, finally exhaled. (Static Ad) Sensitive skin finally exhaled. (UGC Ad) 2,700+ irritants eliminated.
- **Key spoken lines**: None used
- **Visual style**: mixed
- **CTA / offer (if shown)**: None used
- **Narrative arc**: None observable
- **Why shown in this video**: To demonstrate how to multiply a winning ad concept across different formats using the scalable AI system.
- **Speaker's take**: "So we have our headline ad. Now, let's assume that we run this ad and it's a winner, whatever. We want to multiply it into different formats. So I have a winning ad brief that I want to multiply across different format templates. The original brief is below. Here are my format templates... For EACH format template, rewrite the brief to fit that format exactly."
- **Timestamp**: 01:17:37

We want to pull out the ad format, the copy, the product, the layout, the background, the typography, you know, the different visual devices, the color palette, the spacing. [VISUAL: Slide titled "The Core Idea". It explains the process of creating two frames (start and end) and letting the AI connect them.]

Will Sartorius: Because it actually isn't that lazy. Okay, so where I think a lot of folks get wrong, what a lot of folks get wrong here is, I'm going to upload my static into Kling or Veo 3.1, and I'm going to ask it to animate. That doesn't work. Like, what you need to do is you need to create two frames for your static ads. You have your static ad, and then you have the subsequent end frame, or the previous start frame.

I'm going to say I want to do a video. I'm doing frames, like I just mentioned, rather than ingredients. Frames are better than ingredients with Veo, just trust me on that one. I want 9x16, and I want to generate four. The other thing here, you'll notice this prompt is in brackets, it's prompt. What, like, what the fuck is this thing? Don't worry about it. Stop reading prompts. Stop trying to over-engineer your prompts. It will drive you crazy, and it doesn't work. It does work, but it just, there's much better ways to go about this.

[VISUAL: Slide titled "Step 6: Iterate (You Likely Won't One-Shot This)". It shows a "Yikes" version of the animated GIF and a prompt to Claude describing what's wrong and asking for a corrected version.]

Will Sartorius: So, this, you'll notice, is a JSON prompt. The difference between Nano Banana 2 and Veo 3.1 is Nano Banana 2 does a really, really good job with text-based prompts. You don't need to do JSON prompts. Veo 3.1 is a little different. In order for it to adhere sort of frame by frame, you need to do JSON prompts. You can sort of equate Veo 3.1 to Nano Banana Pro, the version before Nano Banana 2, where it's making a lot of assumptions, and we don't want it to make a lot of assumptions. We want to tell it verbatim, this is what I want to have happen. And that's why you're giving it the JSON prompt. And, you know, your first output may sort of suck. And like, that's okay. You know, this is all part of the process. So, like, unless you sort of one-shot this, which I can probably almost guarantee you won't unless you have a very simple animation, what you want to do is you want to take the GIF that you created, so download the video as a GIF, you know, or download it as an MP4, convert it to a GIF, but no matter what, it needs to be a GIF. If you're if you're working directly in, you know, Google, uh, Flow, you can download it as a GIF directly. They have, that's a great feature they have. You're going to put the ad that you created that was sort of bad into Claude, and you're going to tell it what the problems are with it. So, in this example, you know, we have this, it animates an additional green text. We have these gummy bears that look sort of like nothing like our original gummy bears. Uh, and then there's like this weird animation at the very end.

[VISUAL: Slide titled "Step 7: Bask In Your GIF". It shows the final, corrected animated GIF of the gummy bears lifting the lip gloss.]

Will Sartorius: Um, so I just sort of outlined what the issues were here, and then I said, you know, give me a corrected Veo 3.1 JSON prompt that fixes these issues, and then maintain everything else that worked well, or things that I didn't mention. And eventually, you'll get there. This probably took me two or three tries, but then I got there, you know, and I was actually pretty happy with the end result. So, again, you're going to have to iterate a few times on this. Like, sort of similar to, uh, how Nano Banana Pro was. That's okay. Like, eventually you're going to arrive at something you're happy with. Again, people assume that AI is just going to be immediate, like, this is going to give me a great result. And when it comes to statics, yes, we are there. When it comes to videos and GIFs, we're getting there. Veo 4 comes out in May. This presentation will be obsolete at that point because we're going to be behind. Um, but at the end of the day, uh, just be patient. Just be patient. And like, in animating a static like this, you're, you are effectively, not guaranteeing, but shouldn't use that word. You are taking a winning ad and you are converting it into a new format, so it's going to hit a new audience, uh, with presumably lower CPMs.

[VISUAL: A Q&A question from Derek Daniels is displayed on screen: "If you're working for a large brand with a defined 'Brand Bible' that includes visual identify, target consumer, design codes, etc., do you recommend uploading the entire 'bible' (which could be well over 100 pages) or breaking it up?"]

Evan Lee: So if you're working for a large brand with a defined brand Bible that includes visual identity, target consumer, design codes, etc., do you recommend uploading the entire Bible, which could be over 100 pages, or breaking it up?

Will Sartorius: Great, great question. And this gets down to like sort of a fundamental principle. Uh, Claude doesn't like PDFs very much. You know, when Claude is looking at a PDF, what it's going to do is it's going to screenshot each page, and it's going to effectively, and this is assuming your brand Bible is in a PDF or a PowerPoint. Um, what it's going to do is it's going to go and try to screenshot each one. What I would do as a first step is I would upload the, uh, PDF into Claude, and I would say, can you please convert this to a markdown file? Uh, markdown files are your baseline for everything, .md. Every agent is a markdown file, all the recipe cards, all the templates, they're all markdown files. So, yes, give it the entire thing, but first upload it as a markdown file. And then when you're doing, you know, you're you're doing the brand execution steps, say, you know, I have this markdown file in the folder, and then, yeah, you'll be able to have all that properly in there. Um, but yeah, you can, you can absolutely do it. Especially if you're working in Claude Code, like let's say you build a folder, upload both, you know, upload the the the PowerPoint and the PDF, and then also upload the markdown file. Like, it's going to be scanning through the folder that it's working in, and it's going to try, like based on the prompt I gave you, it's going to be trying to build the most comprehensive, uh, you know, spec cards that's humanly possible.

Evan Lee: And then just, uh, just to hit it home for everybody, when you're talking about markdown files and like after interpreting the PDF, are you then separating it out into each section? Is is it its own markdown file, or does that point doesn't really matter, have it all in one?

Will Sartorius: For the brand guidelines?

Evan Lee: Yes.

Will Sartorius: Um, it doesn't really matter because at the end of the day, what you care the most about is one, like having the information all in one place.
